<div id="review_2469973510" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2469973510" />
    <a title="Jacob Proffitt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1331641-jacob-proffitt"><img alt="Jacob Proffitt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325699498p2/1331641.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2469973510?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Proffitt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Proffitt" href="/user/show/1331641-jacob-proffitt">Jacob Proffitt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380255023" href="/book/show/28368607-the-forgotten-beasts-of-eld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1331641-jacob-proffitt?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1331641-jacob-proffitt?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2469973510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15021847199867042611">I know I've read this before but so long ago that I only got brief glimpses/memories of scenes as I read them. Which is just as well because I doubt very much that I could have appreciated the depth of this story when I read it all those years ago.<br/><br/>This story has a kind of oral/bardic beginning that sets the tone for the lyrical beauty of the rest of the prose. It's part style, part choice of imagery that gives it a kind of mythic flavor that might have been off-putting if the underlying story we</span>
  <span id="freeText15021847199867042611" style="display:none">I know I've read this before but so long ago that I only got brief glimpses/memories of scenes as I read them. Which is just as well because I doubt very much that I could have appreciated the depth of this story when I read it all those years ago.<br /><br />This story has a kind of oral/bardic beginning that sets the tone for the lyrical beauty of the rest of the prose. It's part style, part choice of imagery that gives it a kind of mythic flavor that might have been off-putting if the underlying story weren't so deeply human. I loved it from the start which is interesting because it is quickly apparent that the magic of this world is one of compulsion and control and I have a visceral reaction against such. Sybel has a light touch with her animals, but the narrative doesn't flinch from the truth that she could do the same with humans. Indeed, it's almost a relief when the veil is completely ripped away and you see the true horror of the abuse of that power in such close and personal terms and the desperation it engenders in even the threat if its use.<br /><br />And the story goes deeply into the obsessive darkness inherent in being ruled by hate and revenge. Love and forgiveness are hard pressed, but present, too, rounding out the story of family, love, pain, loss, and power. All of which are big, weighty elements that might have made for a hard read, but I didn't find it so. I think that's because it sticks closely with Sybel and her interactions with others are mostly one-on-one and therefore contained and framed in the personal even though the individual elements are so broad. I kind of loved how all the grand movements of the story are expressed through that smaller perspective of the individual and her relationships with other individuals (as opposed to groups, nations, or representatives of same).<br /><br />I have a particular fondness for Coren—the man playing outside his own league but game to keep trying because he values both the experience and the potential. This imprinted particularly strongly after he lets go of his own hate and learns to respect and then love Sybel. His attachment might have felt more sudden if I didn't understand that draw to powerful women so well, myself. And he sees her at her kindest in regards to Tam and her care of his wounds so it isn't a large step to see further to her care for her forgotten beasts, too. Sybel treats him poorly in <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[her scheming against Drede but I can't think of a way to show her emotional landscape so well as in her neglect of him as she turns more to hate, revenge, and fear <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Anyway, this was a strong five stars from the start. And the ending was a knock out. That last line is simple and yet contains a world of meaning that is possible only by the entire story that precedes it. It's a masterpiece of layered meaning and it completely cemented an already solid five stars. And it joins <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47570.The_Homeward_Bounders" title="The Homeward Bounders by Diana Wynne Jones" rel="nofollow noopener">The Homeward Bounders</a> as the pinnacle of powerful last lines.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15021847199867042611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9301596219665154341">4.5 Stars. <br/><br/>Goodness, this was a great story! <br/><br/>Sybel has grown up mostly in isolation. The daughter of a wizard, she is immensely powerful. Her mountain home is surrounded by a beautiful garden in which reside legendary magical beasts whom she controls with her powers. Her situation makes her emotionally isolated and selfish, but change soon comes in the form of an orphaned infant boy, brought to her by Coren, a soldier. Coren asks her to love and protect the child, Tam, and she agrees. With the h</span>
  <span id="freeText9301596219665154341" style="display:none">4.5 Stars. <br /><br />Goodness, this was a great story! <br /><br />Sybel has grown up mostly in isolation. The daughter of a wizard, she is immensely powerful. Her mountain home is surrounded by a beautiful garden in which reside legendary magical beasts whom she controls with her powers. Her situation makes her emotionally isolated and selfish, but change soon comes in the form of an orphaned infant boy, brought to her by Coren, a soldier. Coren asks her to love and protect the child, Tam, and she agrees. With the help of her beasts and the local hedge witch, Maelga, Sybel raises little Tam and in the process, learns to love. When trouble comes to the mountain and threatens the safety of Tam and the beasts, Sybel must fight to protect herself and those she loves.<br /><br />The many painful lessons of growing up include facing ourselves, correcting our faults, and most importantly, learning to forgive others, even when those who harm us don't ask for our forgiveness. There is an enormous amount of truth in this story and it's told in the most enchanting and lyrical manner possible. A modern classic, this belongs on everyone's shelf.<br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1839147154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1839147154" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4161336791287775663">Patricia McKillip is one of those authors that I’ve always intended to read. I bought a used omnibus of her <i>Riddle-Master of Hed</i> trilogy during my first year of college, which was more than a decade ago. Yet for some reason, I’ve never quite gotten around to reading it, anything else by her. After having now read <i>The Forgotten Beasts of Eld</i>, my interest in her work has been rekindled. <b>This little standalone was a lovely reading experience.</b> And as it referenced her <i>Riddle-Master</i> series multiple t</span>
  <span id="freeText4161336791287775663" style="display:none">Patricia McKillip is one of those authors that I’ve always intended to read. I bought a used omnibus of her <i>Riddle-Master of Hed</i> trilogy during my first year of college, which was more than a decade ago. Yet for some reason, I’ve never quite gotten around to reading it, anything else by her. After having now read <i>The Forgotten Beasts of Eld</i>, my interest in her work has been rekindled. <b>This little standalone was a lovely reading experience.</b> And as it referenced her <i>Riddle-Master</i> series multiple times, that series has climbed closer to the top of my TBR list.<br /><blockquote>“How much that name means to you—memory, knowledge, experience. There is not one possession more truly, irrevocably yours.”</blockquote><br /><b>As I’ve aged, I’ve developed a greater appreciation for quiet novels.</b> Peter S. Beagle, Juliet Marrillier, and Robin McKinley all write fantasy stories that are subtle in just about every way, which could result in reader detachment when not properly handled. The action, the character development, the world building, the philosophy, and even the prose are all almost ethereal in a way when done well. <b>These types of books remind me of pastel watercolors in a world of neons and jewel tones; not as showy, perhaps, but a moment of calm in the midst of a tempest.</b> And that’s exactly how McKillip’s writing felt to me. There is something fairy-talesque about writing of this caliber paired with quiet storytelling, and it was really lovely to experience.<br /><blockquote>“What do you think love is—a thing to startle from the heart like a bird at every shout or blow? You can fly from me, high as you choose in your darkness, but you will see me always beneath you, no matter how far away, with my face turned to you. My heart is in your heart.”</blockquote><br />Sybel is from a line of wizards who can draw mythical beasts to themselves by discovering their true names. She is happy in her solitude with only these beasts for company until a man shows up on her doorstep with a baby in his arms. After agreeing to raise the child, her life finds a new normalcy until the boy’s father comes looking for him. From there, Sybel’s life is turned upside down as she finds herself caught between powerful men who have grown to hate one another.<br /><blockquote>“I have fought for myself—and fought myself. But there is not joy in that. It is only when I am with you that I know, deep in me, how to laugh, and there is no one, no one who can teach me that but you.”</blockquote><br /><b>There is something really romantic about this story, even before any romantic involvement occurs.</b> The lyrical writing mixed with the lovely setting definitely had something to do with it, as did the beasts themselves. I loved learning about the beasts in Sybel’s life, and how they had become just as attached to her as she had to them. But when the actual romance began to take root, the slow burn of it was enchanting. <b>I also tend to love unexplained magic systems. These magic systems often involve the magic of true names, which I think is a really interesting similarity.</b> From a Judeo-Christian perspective, the giving of true names has been one of our callings since the creation of Adam, and I find the portrayal of that naming in fantasy novels fascinating.<br /><blockquote>“I have many people who know my name, but only one or two or three that know who it belongs to.”</blockquote><br /><i>The Forgotten Beasts of Eld</i> is as lovely as it is brief. As I said above, I’ve developed quite a deep appreciation for and fondness of fantasy authors who choose to tell bright, quiet stories instead of embracing the breakneck action and grim-darkness that so populate the genre. <b>If you need a book that will help you take a deep breath in the midst of your feverishly-paced life, I heartily recommend a visit to Sybel’s world.</b><br /><br /><i>You can find <a href="https://wp.me/pa0WBY-1hs" rel="nofollow noopener"> this</a> review and more at <a href="http://novelnotions.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions</a>. </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4161336791287775663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2101584497" />
    <a title="Sheila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6810645-sheila"><img alt="Sheila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460570342p2/6810645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2101584497?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sheila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sheila" href="/user/show/6810645-sheila">Sheila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781616962777" href="/book/show/34724787-the-forgotten-beasts-of-eld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6810645-sheila?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6810645-sheila?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2101584497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12532178104078257571">5 stars--it was amazing.<br/><br/>A story about this book: I first read it when I was 11 or 12. My local library at the time didn't have a YA section (in fact, I'm not sure the concept of YA fiction existed at the time, though that would change soon--by the time I was in high school, the library had a separate section for teens). The library's juvenile book section was arranged with picture books on one side of the room, middle-reader chapter books in the middle of the room, and one free-standing shelf of</span>
  <span id="freeText12532178104078257571" style="display:none">5 stars--it was amazing.<br /><br />A story about this book: I first read it when I was 11 or 12. My local library at the time didn't have a YA section (in fact, I'm not sure the concept of YA fiction existed at the time, though that would change soon--by the time I was in high school, the library had a separate section for teens). The library's juvenile book section was arranged with picture books on one side of the room, middle-reader chapter books in the middle of the room, and one free-standing shelf of "older juvenile" books on the other side of the room. <br /><br />This book was on that free-standing shelf. I'd already read a good portion of the middle-reader books and was starting to make forays into that exciting new shelf of <i>big kid</i> books.<br /><br />I'm not sure why this book was there. I wouldn't classify it as juvenile/YA fiction. But there it was, and I read it, and loved it so much I probably reread it three times in the next couple years. I already loved fantasy books, but after these early readings, I started to seek out McKillip novels--something I still do today. She's one of my favorites.<br /><br />Now I think that 11 was probably too young to read this novel, but at the time I loved the strange and fascinating beasts. I wanted to have long silver hair and be a wizard in a tower, surrounded by magical creatures. The more symbolic elements--about what it means to be a woman, how power differs for men and women, the destructive power of both hatred and love--went over my head. Thankfully.<br /><br />Because there's one scene in this book when, read as an adult, is absolutely harrowing. Hearing the heroine (who's entirely self-reliant and powerful) beg was devastating. It upset me. (All good fiction should evoke an emotional reaction, so it was a pleasant sort of upsetting!) <br /><br />When I saw this book re-released and available on NetGalley, I snatched it up. I was interested in seeing if I'd still love it. The answer is yes: absolutely and whole-heartedly. This book holds up to my early infatuation and more. The prose is gorgeous (McKillip is one of the best), and the story is gripping. I still want to be a silver-haired wizard in a tower with magical beasts. I guess some things never change.<br /><br />I received this review copy from the publisher on NetGalley. Thanks for the opportunity to read and review; I appreciate it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12532178104078257571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText7889468927147716720" style="display:none">I may be an odd reader for this book, but it captured my imagination best in the first half of the book and less so in the second.<br /><br />That being said, I thought it was a well-written, sometimes mildly poetical romance with all the old, familiar fantasy tropes. Mythical creatures are really mostly a side-issue. It's really about hearts. Big surprise, right? It is a romance. :)<br /><br />All said, I enjoyed it well as a mild entertainment. Maybe my jaded reading eyes have just seen too much like this to get overawed. But prop where prop is due: the fantasy realm by the mid seventies was mostly going the strange route and hadn't been revamped to the kind that feels more like THIS.<br /><br />We've seen a lot of this since then, however. Enjoyable, regardless.<br /><br /></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2039801968" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2039801968" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2039801968?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2039801968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2637323721907451503">Rating: 3.5* of five<br/><br/>I read this book in 1975. It was impressive to my teenaged self, but was the beginning of the end for me and fantasy as a primary reading genre. <br/><br/>Beautifully written, richly textured, full of those lovely small moments that demonstrate deft and economical world-building. Also larded through with moments of profound insight for my inexperienced self:<br/><blockquote>What do you think love is—a thing to startle from the heart like a bird at every shout or blow? You can fly from me, high as you c</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2637323721907451503" style="display:none">Rating: 3.5* of five<br /><br />I read this book in 1975. It was impressive to my teenaged self, but was the beginning of the end for me and fantasy as a primary reading genre. <br /><br />Beautifully written, richly textured, full of those lovely small moments that demonstrate deft and economical world-building. Also larded through with moments of profound insight for my inexperienced self:<br /><blockquote>What do you think love is—a thing to startle from the heart like a bird at every shout or blow? You can fly from me, high as you choose into your darkness, but you will see me always beneath you, no matter how far away, with my face turned to you. My heart is in your heart. I gave it to you with my name that night and you are its guardian, to treasure it, or let it wither and die. I do not understand you. I am angry with you. I am hurt and helpless, but nothing will fill the ache of the hollowness in me where your name would echo if I lost you.</blockquote><br />It wouldn't hit me how precisely this passage describes parenthood, as well as other profound states of love, until I got there myself. When I first read it I got a shivery foreboding of how cold passion can be. Thank you, Author McKillip, for the forewarning that I wish I'd heeded better.<br /><br />But in the end, I felt I was being womaned. McKillip seemed to me to be speaking to women and, if some random man chose to listen in, that's okay but you're not the point of the conversation. That men were the *subject* of the conversation, and the uncomfortable implications of that, didn't hit me for years. The end of that process in my reading life was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/402045.The_Mists_of_Avalon__Avalon___1_" title="The Mists of Avalon (Avalon, #1) by Marion Zimmer Bradley" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mists of Avalon</a>. I've said it before: I could feel myself growing a uterus as I read Bradley's book. I don't want a uterus. Fantasy novels of that era all seemed to be determined to reassign my gender, and I was and am very much not down with that.<br /><br />I'm still not a committed fantasy novel reader, though I no longer reject out of hand the mere notion of reading one. The rise of urban fantasy has brought me back to the chairs right by the door under the fantasy tent. I still don't much care for majgickq and teenagers as main characters require some serious persuading to induce me to pick up and read a book. But it's at least possible to get me near them.<br /><br />Thanks, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/55066255-a-dawes&quot;" rel="nofollow noopener">Doc</a>, for the reminder that this book left its pawprints on my inner manuscript. It's good to flip back the pages once in a way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2637323721907451503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1583934890028033880">Dude, I'm glad this was not the first McKillip book I ever read. I've loved everything I've read by her before, but this was terrible. Which is too bad, since I always heard it was so great. But I definitely wouldn't have given her other books a chance if I'd come across this one first.<br/><br/>Well, okay, <i>terrible</i> might be a little strong. There are well-written parts. Still, skill matters little when I hated a) the characters, b) the plot, and c) seriously, every single character. And parts of it <i>weren</i></span>
  <span id="freeText1583934890028033880" style="display:none">Dude, I'm glad this was not the first McKillip book I ever read. I've loved everything I've read by her before, but this was terrible. Which is too bad, since I always heard it was so great. But I definitely wouldn't have given her other books a chance if I'd come across this one first.<br /><br />Well, okay, <i>terrible</i> might be a little strong. There are well-written parts. Still, skill matters little when I hated a) the characters, b) the plot, and c) seriously, every single character. And parts of it <i>weren't</i> well done. It definitely feels like an early work; the style I expect in her writing isn't quite there, it has a tendency towards melodrama, the pacing is terrible, and the characters - and especially their relationships - are way underdeveloped. I always feel like there's a bit of a sense of detachment in her books, and I generally like that, but I feel it times a thousand here and it doesn't work. It didn't let me connect to the characters at all; therefore, I couldn't have cared less about them.<br /><br />I most especially hated Sybel though. Not a good sign when your most loathed part of a book is its main character. But there was absolutely nothing about her that I liked. I kind of wanted to punch her. A lot. And, like, every dude wanted her the moment they saw her. Ugh.<br /><br />I really tried to like this, but I just really, really didn't.<br /><br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129802023" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9062175659543644242">Enter a land where wise old boars speak, as well as falcons, lions and cats. A land filled with sorcery, beauty and evil . . .<br/><br/>Known as the ice white lady, Sybil was raised to live to care only for the mythical beasts under her control - powerful, beautiful and wise, and feared by man - she knows none of the ways of men and prefers to keep it that way. Until one day, when a young babe is left in her arms, and she learns to love, and gets entangled in a War she wants no part of.<br/><br/>THE FORGOTTEN BEAST</span>
  <span id="freeText9062175659543644242" style="display:none">Enter a land where wise old boars speak, as well as falcons, lions and cats. A land filled with sorcery, beauty and evil . . .<br /><br />Known as the ice white lady, Sybil was raised to live to care only for the mythical beasts under her control - powerful, beautiful and wise, and feared by man - she knows none of the ways of men and prefers to keep it that way. Until one day, when a young babe is left in her arms, and she learns to love, and gets entangled in a War she wants no part of.<br /><br />THE FORGOTTEN BEASTS OF ELD is a treasure worth seeking out. It's an imaginative story of love, betrayal and forgiveness, filled with intriguing characters you won't soon forget. The dialogue is sparse and the tale is short but there is an abundant amount of character growth and plenty of plot points to ponder. There's also enough magic and surprises to keep those pages turning. I never knew quite where the story was going to lead. I'm now on the hunt for her other work.<br /><br />This book is a World Fantasy Award winning novel for a good reason. </span>

<div id="review_2626243083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2626243083" />
    <a title="Kalin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/741149-kalin"><img alt="Kalin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502630193p2/741149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2626243083?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalin" href="/user/show/741149-kalin">Kalin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575077652" href="/book/show/1681934.The_Forgotten_Beasts_of_Eld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/741149-kalin?shelf=reviewed-in-english">reviewed-in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2626243083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2432686373083729970">This tale teeters on the brink of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2235628262' rel='nofollow noopener'>that enchantment</a> that won me over to Patricia A. McKillip's writing. At times, I was afraid it would keel over and shatter. But in the end, it soared.<br/><br/>Also, as I read through my notes, I realize it has lent its magic to a great miracle in my own life. When I wrote the first note, I was beginning to look into adopting a child. As I finish writing them, a child and her mother have begun taking me in.<br/><br/>But that's another story and will be told another time. ;) Now foc</span>
  <span id="freeText2432686373083729970" style="display:none">This tale teeters on the brink of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2235628262" rel="nofollow noopener">that enchantment</a> that won me over to Patricia A. McKillip's writing. At times, I was afraid it would keel over and shatter. But in the end, it soared.<br /><br />Also, as I read through my notes, I realize it has lent its magic to a great miracle in my own life. When I wrote the first note, I was beginning to look into adopting a child. As I finish writing them, a child and her mother have begun taking me in.<br /><br />But that's another story and will be told another time. ;) Now focus on this one; it's good enough itself.<br /><br />~ So I want to be a foster parent? I'd better be prepared.<br /><br /><blockquote>Tam came back after a long while. He went to her silently, crept close to her under the fall of her long hair as though he were a small child again. For a long time he was silent. Then he said softly, “Nyl ran ahead, and told his mother what lie we told the King. So—he left unsure of me. Sybel—”<br />She felt him trembling. “What, Tam?”<br />“He—we talked a little. He—” His head dropped suddenly onto her knees. Her hand moved gently over his hair as he cried, his hands crumpling her skirt. He quieted finally, and she lifted his face between her hands.<br />“My Tam, it is not such a terrible thing for a boy to want his father.”<br />“But I love you, too! I do not want to leave you, but—I wanted—I wanted—to say I was his son and watch his eyes—to see if he was pleased with me. We talked of Ter—he said it was a marvelous thing that I was not afraid to hunt with him.” He stared up at her, heavy-eyed, desperate. “I do not know what to do. I want to stay and I want to go. Sybel— If I go—would you come?”<br />“But Tam, what would I do with the animals?”<br />“You must come! Bring the animals— Sybel, he would want you to come— Coren wanted you— You could do things for him—”<br />“Against the Lords of Sirle?” she said a little sharply and he was silent. “That is what he would use me for.”<br />“I do not care what he would use you for,” Tam whispered. “I want you to come.”<br />She shook her head, her eyes dark. “No, my Tam. I will do anything for you but that. You have your life to make and I have mine. I am sorry, but you must choose between us. I will always be here in this mountain when you have need of me— No, do not cry, my Tam—” She smiled, her own eyes wet, and wiped the tears from his face with her fingers. “You were so small and soft once,” she whispered, “and you fit so surely in my arms... I did not know then that you would grow up to hurt me so.”</blockquote><br /><br />~ Is that the crux of the conflict?<br /><br /><blockquote>Drede was silent. Sybel rose and drew back the curtain. The King’s eyes leaped to her face; she saw shame in them, torment, and beneath them the icy glaze of fear. She stood still a moment, her hand on the curtain. Then she went to him and knelt at his feet.<br />“Please,” she whispered. “Please. I will do whatever you ask. I will marry you. I will put the Sirle Lords under your power. I will raise Tam, and I will bear you sons. I will never argue with you; I will obey you without question. But do not let him take my will from me. Do not let him change my mind. It is a terrible thing, more terrible than if you killed me here, now. I would rather you do that. There is a part of me, like a white-winged falcon, free, proud, wild, a soaring thing that goes its own way seeking the bright stars and the sun. If you kill that white bird, I will be earthbound, bound in the patterns of men, with no words of my own, no actions of my own. I will take that bird for you, cage it. Only let it live.”<br />Drede lifted one hand, covered his eyes. Then he knelt before Sybel and took her hands in his hands, holding them tightly. “Sybel, I am helpless in this matter. I want you, but I am afraid of you—afraid of that white bird.”<br />“I promise—I promise—”<br />“No, listen to me. I have been—I have lived afraid always of those I hold in power. I have been threatened by my lords, betrayed by those I loved, until there is no one I can speak the truth to without being afraid. My own people, the ones I should trust, I look into their eyes, their secret, expressionless eyes, and I suspect them, I fear their treachery. I am alone. Tamlorn is the one thing in this world I trust and love. You, I could love, and perhaps trust, but I must be certain of you, Sybel.”<br />She said, her mouth dry, “You—cannot ever be certain of those you love—that they will not hurt you, even loving you. But to make me certain to love you, will be to take away any love I might give you freely. That white bird’s name is Sybel. If you kill it, I will die and a ghost will look out of my eyes. Trust me. Let me live, and trust me.”</blockquote><br /><br />The opposite of love is not hatred. Neither is it indifference.<br /><br />Some of you know what it is, see it. Yes?<br /><br />~ The clash between Sybel and Coren in Chapter 11 is a fine specimen of a quarrel between lovers--between partners, or close friends--and leads up to this:<br /><br /><blockquote>“(...) Sybel, I do not understand this game you are playing. Do you think if I know you, I will fear you? Cease to love you?”<br />“Yes,” she whispered. “As you are doing now.”<br />He gripped her suddenly, shook her, hurting her. “That is not true! What do you think love is—a thing to startle from the heart like a bird at every shout or blow? You can fly from me, high as you choose into your darkness, but you will see me always beneath you, no matter how far away, with my face turned to you. My heart is in your heart. I gave it to you with my name that night and you are its guardian, to treasure it, or let it wither and die. I do not understand you. I am angry with you. I am hurt and helpless, but nothing would fill the ache of the hollowness in me where your name would echo if I lost you.”</blockquote><br /><br />Now let's see how they're going to untangle this mess ....<br /><br />~ Judging by the publication date, McKillip must have been at most 25 when she wrote <i>The Forgotten Beasts</i>. Is that why some interactions between Sybel and Coren feel subtly immature--not explicitly enough to put the problem into words but still tearing at the hems of my own experience? Or, if the characters are in their 20s (I rarely remember these things), is their behavior exactly right (in its wrongness ;)? Or is there something else, something that will yet reveal itself and confirm another nagging suspicion: that McKillip had a precocious understanding of human beings from the very start? Like Peter S. Beagle in <i>A Fine and Private Place</i> and <i>The Last Unicorn</i>?<br /><br />I have a chapter and a half left to find out.<br /><br />~ So. They <i>are</i> growing wise, after all. :)<br /><br />And I'm growing more and more thrilled about the journey that lies ahead of me, across all those books, the tales which will grow out of this one and the next and the one after that ....</span>
  <a data-text-id="2432686373083729970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2950641700" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2950641700" />
    <a title="Kaa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19931478-kaa"><img alt="Kaa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539408167p2/19931478.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2950641700?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaa" href="/user/show/19931478-kaa">Kaa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/34330548-the-forgotten-beasts-of-eld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19931478-kaa?shelf=tsffs-books">tsffs-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19931478-kaa?shelf=group-buddy-reads">group-buddy-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19931478-kaa?shelf=seven-kingdoms">seven-kingdoms</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2950641700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16597027786347871256">I love fairy tales, especially stories about loneliness and anger and coming to terms with the dark places within ourselves. Forgotten Beasts is a beautiful example of this genre. The storytelling style was perfect for story, flowing along with the slight distance of myth. The characters are all figures of legend, rather than flesh-and-blood people (or animals, as case may be), but that works for me in this kind of fairy tale, giving it a feeling of history and place within some broader landscap</span>
  <span id="freeText16597027786347871256" style="display:none">I love fairy tales, especially stories about loneliness and anger and coming to terms with the dark places within ourselves. Forgotten Beasts is a beautiful example of this genre. The storytelling style was perfect for story, flowing along with the slight distance of myth. The characters are all figures of legend, rather than flesh-and-blood people (or animals, as case may be), but that works for me in this kind of fairy tale, giving it a feeling of history and place within some broader landscape of folklore. This story and the style of telling remind me, actually, of the Wizard of Earthsea - the quiet tale of a young wizard struggling for their future with their own pride and fear, told by someone who sees that conflicted young person but who also knows the wiser adult they are in the process of becoming. A lovely story, and one I am likely to continue to think of and reread.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16597027786347871256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8087.Deerskin"><img alt="Deerskin" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1405919760l/8087._SX98_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/DIxaepNBmzdsH2C/FtyJ0zV57zAK2xG5eXBHlDO1bLyGZBh0TqtbvHtaZjsfN2HvCFt+Irai++TXVhpVv0ngA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HIx55t4ClODxsxTpTMjx3Xox9FC6uzJX+xhOKL432psSp9ju5XU/4t3uAPNc7159isV+FPBK1f6NoNdfuAQoqQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25.Patricia_A_McKillip">About Patricia A. McKillip</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25.Patricia_A_McKillip"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1220752490p3/25.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25.Patricia_A_McKillip">Patricia A. McKillip
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,168
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9530602323688711487">Patricia Anne McKillip is an American author of fantasy and science fiction novels, distinguished by lyrical, delicate prose and careful attention to detail and characterization. She is a past winner of the World Fantasy Award and Locus Award, and she lives in Oregon. Most of her recent novels have cover paintings by Kinuko Y. Craft. She is married to David Lunde, a poet.<br/><br/>According to Fantasy Book </span>
  <span id="freeText9530602323688711487" style="display:none">Patricia Anne McKillip is an American author of fantasy and science fiction novels, distinguished by lyrical, delicate prose and careful attention to detail and characterization. She is a past winner of the World Fantasy Award and Locus Award, and she lives in Oregon. Most of her recent novels have cover paintings by Kinuko Y. Craft. She is married to David Lunde, a poet.<br /><br />According to Fantasy Book Review, Patricia McKillip grew up in Oregon, England, and Germany, and received a Bachelor of Arts (English) in 1971 and a Master of Arts in 1973 from San Jose State University.<br /><br />McKillip's stories usually take place in a setting similar to the Middle Ages. There are forests, castles, and lords or kings, minstrels, tinkers and wizards. Her writing usually puts her characters in situations involving mysterious powers that they don't understand. Many of her characters aren't even sure of their own ancestry. Music often plays an important role. Love between family members is also important in McKillip's writing, although members of her families often disagree.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9530602323688711487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
